Chriss will serve his suspension tonight when the Cavaliers visit the Philadelphia 76ers at Well Fargo Center, while Ibaka will begin serving his suspension on Thursday when the Raptors host the Los Angeles Lakers."},{"id":35051,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/nba-legends-missing-out-playoffs","date":"2019-03-12","title":"Missing the playoffs not typical for NBA legends","content":" Either LeBron James is going to get distracted by his outside ventures in Hollywood this summer, or the rest of the NBA might be in trouble. The crossroads is clearly marked, the only uncertainty being which path James takes. In likely missing the NBA playoffs for the first time in 14 years, will James lose the focus so essential to his day job and plunge headlong into the entertainment and philanthropic pursuits that intrigue him and fill up so many of the hours not demanded by basketball? Or, with two additional months freed up by missing not just the postseason but The Finals for the first time since 2010 , will James up his gym time and challenge himself physically and mentally to come back healthier, stronger and better for 2019-20, the season during which he\u2019ll turn 35 years old? At least one of James\u2019 old cronies expects the latter. As Kevin Love told Cleveland.com recently , \u201cJust having that break, being able to reassess and come back really, really highly motivated is going to be big for him. If you get \u2018Bron highly motivated anything can happen.\u201d First, of course, comes a period of adjustment in processing a season without any playoff possibilities. That hasn\u2019t been part of the James brand since 2005, his second year in the NBA, back when he still couldn\u2019t legally buy an adult beverage. Even if his body craves a longer layoff after eight straight Finals trips, during which he averaged nearly 97 regular season and postseason appearances \u2014 cramming in the equivalent of two extra seasons \u2014 it figures to be disorienting to be idled when more than half the league keeps going come mid-April. It also will bump James down a rung among the game\u2019s legendary players, as measured by how frequently or infrequently they failed to lead their teams into the playoffs. From a collection of 30 of the NBA\u2019s best players ever, nine never missed a chance to qualify for the big spring tournament. Eight more did it in all but one or two of the years they played, a group that includes G.O.A.T. candidates Wilt Chamberlain, Kareem Abdul-Jabbar and Michael Jordan. James, once his Lakers are mathematically eliminated from this year\u2019s field of 16, will slip to the next tier of Hall of Fame-worthy players who failed to steer their clubs home three times. Then there\u2019s the group who wound up outside looking in four or more times for whatever reasons. In all, this group of 30 greats have logged 459 individual seasons, only 65 of which ended without a playoff berth. That\u2019s how rare and out of character this 2018-19 Lakers season has been, not just for James\u2019 legacy but in comparison to the best of the best. Here is a look at the ones that got away:  First time, every time (9)  Elgin Baylor: 13 seasons, no misses, 1.000 He missed the playoffs in 1971 due to injury and retired nine games in the following season, but the Lakers never failed to qualify in his one-franchise career. Larry Bird: 13 seasons, no misses, 1.000 Bird missed the postseason in 1989, the year he played in only six games, though the Celtics did not.  Bob Cousy: 13 seasons, no misses, 1.000 We\u2019re not going to hold the 1969-70 season \u2013 when Cousy played limited minutes in seven games as Cincinnati\u2019s player-coach, six years after retiring \u2013 against him. Tim Duncan: 19 years, no misses, 1.000 No one in league history has gone 20-for-20, so Duncan and Karl Malone are the best of our 1.000 hitters. Duncan missed the 2000 playoffs due to injury and that was too much for the defending-champion Spurs to overcome, getting ousted by Phoenix in four. Julius Erving: 11 seasons, no misses, 1.000 And that\u2019s just his NBA years. The Doctor went 5-for-5 in the ABA, twice winning titles with the Nets and averaging 31.1 points and 12.9 boards in those five postseasons. Magic Johnson: 13 seasons, no misses, 1.000 This even includes his limited 1995-96 season, a forgettable 32-game comeback after four years of retirement. Karl Malone: 19 seasons, no misses, 1.000 The Mailman\u2019s running buddy, John Stockton, never missed, either, in his 19-year Hall of Fame career. Bill Russell: 13 seasons, no misses, 1.000 The idea of this guy and his Boston team sitting out a postseason is unimaginable. Jerry West: 13 seasons, no misses, 1.000 West was such a noble and, with the exception of the Lakers\u2019 title in \u201972, ill-fated playoff performer that he was named Finals MVP in 1970 from the losing side. The asterisk club (3)  Wilt Chamberlain: 14 seasons, one miss (1963), .929 In any conversation about G.O.A.T. candidates, Chamberlain\u2019s teams missed the playoffs fewer times than either Jordan\u2019s (twice) or James (three). Scottie Pippen: 17 seasons, one miss (2004), .941 Six of Pippen\u2019s postseason appearances came when he wasn\u2019t a teammate of Michael Jordan. David Robinson: 14 seasons, one miss (1997), .929 The Spurs got swept from the first round when Robinson couldn\u2019t participate in the 1992 postseason. When he was hurt again in 1997, San Antonio missed the playoffs entirely but landed Duncan in a stroke of lottery luck. Fool me twice\u2026 (5)  Kareem Abdul-Jabbar: 20 seasons, two misses (1975, \u201976), .900 This was not a happy Captain in his post-Oscar, pre-Magic run through his prime of two misses and five postseasons (1975-79) without a Finals appearance. Michael Jordan: 15 seasons, two misses (2002, \u201903), .867 Jordan\u2019s Bulls era is unsullied, but his return to action with Washington was a business decision that scratched a personal itch rather than a winning strategy. Shaquille O\u2019Neal: 19 seasons, two misses (1993, \u201909), .895 The No. 8 seed in the East in 2009 won 39 games, but Phoenix\u2019s 46 weren\u2019t enough in the West that season. O\u2019Neal also missed as a rookie with Orlando (1992-93). Bob Pettit: 11 seasons, two misses, .818 Pettit\u2019s Hawks teams missed the playoffs in his 1955 Rookie of the Year season and again in 1962, despite his 31.1 ppg and 18.7 rpg. Dwyane Wade: 16 seasons, two misses (2008, \u201915), .875 Miami held the No. 8 spot in the East as this was written, so it counts among his 14 trips to the postseason thus far. Three strikes, you\u2019re out! (7)  Charles Barkley: 16 seasons, three misses (1988, \u201992, \u201900), .813 Barkley\u2019s three stops \u2013 in Philadelphia, Phoenix and Houston \u2013 couldn\u2019t sustain playoff excellence enough to earn him a ring. Rick Barry: 10 seasons, three misses (1966, \u201974, \u201978), .700 Barry\u2019s NBA\/ABA\/NBA again career included a personal playoff whiff with the Oakland Oaks in 1969, thanks to torn knee ligaments, though the team went 60-18 and won that league\u2019s championship. Stephen Curry: 10 seasons, three misses (2010, \u201911, \u201912), .700 People tend to forget how much of the Warriors\u2019 dark days Curry saw. They were a cumulative 60 games under .500 his first three seasons. Kevin Durant: 12 seasons, three misses (2008, \u201909, \u201915), .750 The SuperSonics\/Thunder were 43-121 his first two seasons, then missed again at 45-37 when Durant was limited to just 27 appearances by a foot injury. John Havlicek: 16 seasons, three misses (1970, \u201971, \u201978), .813 Won six rings with Russell, then bridged post-Russell gap to win two more in \u201974 and \u201976. LeBron James: 16 seasons, three misses (2004, \u201905, \u201919), .813 Lakers fans are hoping this is like Kobe Bryant\u2019s blip in \u201905 rather than his run of three straight lottery finishes prior to retirement. Hakeem Olajuwon: 18 seasons, three misses (1992, \u201900, \u201901), .833 Olajuwon\u2019s first year without a playoff appearance nearly got him traded. His last two signaled the end was near. To infinity and beyond (6)  Kobe Bryant: 20 seasons, four misses (2005, \u201914, \u201915, \u201916), .800 Bryant\u2019s extended off-ramp from his Hall of Fame career included three early offseasons. Kevin Garnett: 21 seasons, six misses (1996, \u201905, \u201906, \u201907, \u201915, \u201916), .714 The 2005-07 hole in the prime of Garnett\u2019s career, when Minnesota couldn\u2019t assemble a worthy supporting cast around him, is unique among the game\u2019s all-time greats. Moses Malone: 19 seasons, seven misses (1978, \u201986, \u201990, \u201992, \u201993, \u201994, \u201995), .632 Play for enough franchises \u2013 and Malone did, at seven \u2013 and you\u2019re bound to end up on some clunkers. Most of his misses came from ages 34-39. Dirk Nowitzki: 21 seasons, six misses (1999, \u201900, \u201913, \u201917, \u201918, \u201919), .714 Nowitzki has kind of a Kobe thing going on, his Hall of Fame-worthy greatness no longer enough to boost into the playoffs the only franchise for which he\u2019s played. Oscar Robertson: 14 seasons, four misses (1961, \u201968, \u201969, \u201970), .714 It was Robertson\u2019s final years in Cincinnati that prepared him for his late-career run (four playoffs, two Finals, one ring) with Kareem Abdul-Jabbar in Milwaukee. Isiah Thomas: 13 seasons, four misses (1982, \u201983, \u201993, \u201994), .692 The Hall of Fame point guard missed in his first two and his last two seasons and, more specifically, never reached the postseason without Chuck Daly as coach. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. In this sense, these are the dead giveaways about the current state of the Boston Celtics: Kyrie Irving isn\u2019t bringing down the mood in the room anymore. Coach Brad Stevens doesn\u2019t look like a confused coach. And the outside noise is quieter and so is the feeling of doom. When the Celtics took the flight to California on March 4, they were thankful for the Los Angeles Lakers \u2026 or else they\u2019d be Team Dysfunction. Seven days later, they\u2019re returning home refreshed and frisky, almost as if they spent a week on the West Coast vacationing instead of working. Don\u2019t put too much weight in their 140-115 loss Monday to the LA Clippers. They were ambushed by the better-than-you-think Clippers and played without Jayson Tatum (who sat out with a sore right shoulder). Any coach or veteran player will tell you that winning the last game of a road trip might be the toughest task of an NBA schedule. Well before last night\u2019s buzzer sounded, the goal was already accomplished. The Celtics had righted and re-positioned themselves as one of four teams with a realistic shot at winning the East. What we\u2019ve learned about the Celtics is that they\u2019re not the lost team of late February \u2026 and probably not completely free of problems either. Instead, the answer is simple: the season\u2019s too long. Over the course of 82 games, even contenders get face-palmed \u2014 just ask the Warriors about their 10 home losses. Yet the one thing that remains constant through it all? You\u2019re still a contender. And that\u2019s what the Celtics were during the low moments and still are after this 3-1 road trip. The playoffs \u2014 and nothing else \u2014 will determine the truth. \u201cWe\u2019ve been all over the map,\u201d Stevens said, \u201clike a lot of teams.\u201d Yes, it got shaky there for a minute if you dwell about the small picture. The Celtics are 41-27 \u2026 does that sound or look like a problem? \u201cWe were successful on this trip,\u201d said Irving, who stopped brooding \u2014 temporarily, anyway. \u201cNow we go home to handle our business. We\u2019ve got to keep it moving. Keep winning.\u201d The Celtics are being held, and holding themselves, to a high standard because of last season\u2019s run to the Eastern Conference finals without Irving. This team, well coached and well-assembled, flourished minus a top-10 player. Simple mathematics say Celtics-plus-Irving equals heavyweight this season. Yet it hasn\u2019t been so simple. For a variety of reasons, the Celtics never managed to sit atop the East and, at times, appeared disjointed on the floor. For all his deserved applause as a young coach, Stevens looked surprisingly helpless while trying to develop cohesion, consistency and harmony. After a superb 2018 playoffs, Tatum hasn\u2019t matched that level this season. And Gordon Hayward is still slow to return to Utah Jazz-era Hayward. And so, the Celtics surrendered the attic in the East to the Milwaukee Bucks and slowly drifted downward. Not out of playoff position, but it felt that way. The low point came in losing six of their eight games before this trip. They looked overmatched against the Bucks, Toronto Raptors, Houston Rockets and Portland Trail Blazers during that stretch. And didn\u2019t the Celtics belong in that group? Chemistry was at risk and Irving handled the burden poorly within and beyond the locker room. A true leader uplifts his teammates, bravely faces questions and carries himself with great composure in tough times. Irving turned sour, seemed to deflect blame and mostly wanted nothing to do with the duties of leadership. But give him a pass on this. Great players aren\u2019t necessarily great leaders (and it\u2019s probably time we stop insisting they be). Plus, the Celtics\u2019 lackluster play then would\u2019ve tested anyone\u2019s patience. \u201cI\u2019ve been through too many battles to hang onto the same emotions game after game,\u201d Irving said. Truth be told, the Celtics have just as much of a chance to win the East title as the Bucks, Raptors or Philadelphia 76ers. They\u2019ve got a superstar in Irving and solid supporting pieces. Both Stevens and his roster are playoff tested, as few key players on the Bucks, Raptors or Sixers have gone deeper in the playoffs than the Celtics\u2019 have. Plus, the Celtics believe better days are coming, and that\u2019s half the battle, regaining that confidence. \u201cFor the most part we played great basketball on this trip,\u201d said Jaylen Brown. \u201cWe\u2019ve been away from home a long time. We wanted to get all four games on this trip, but it\u2019ll be good to go home and take the next step. We\u2019ve just got to pick it up. And you do that by winning. Winning stands out.\u201d Brown says the right formula is staying true to your identity, something that escaped the Celtics for a while. \u201cWhen we play with tempo and pace, it\u2019s tough for anyone to beat us,\u201d he said. \u201cWhen we slow it down \u2026\u201d Sometimes when players on a team is on the road, they can\u2019t escape each other. They\u2019re at the same hotel, sometimes eat at the same spot, and that can have a unifying effect. Perhaps that has been the case in California for the Celtics. \u201cThe way we played and stayed together, it showed,\u201d said Al Horford. \u201cWe played with a purpose and want to continue that way. It\u2019s about consistency, and we did a lot of that on this trip.\u201d Half of Boston\u2019s remaining 14 games are against teams currently .500 or better. In April, four of their five games are against teams with losing records. At 1 1\/2 games out of third place \u2014 the Celtics\u2019 ceiling \u2014 that goal could still be reached to close out 2018-19. That\u2019s why anyone who made a final statement on the Celtics prior this trip was being too hasty. The NBA season is long, and rough patches are expected. The Celtics, perhaps, have hit their final one. It\u2019s probably wise, though, that they don\u2019t believe that and fall into a trap. \u201cIt\u2019s not like we solved a puzzle after three (wins),\u201d said Stevens. \u201cWe have to get better.\u201d Yes, they do. But same goes for any team that calls itself a contender. The pack is just too tight and the competition too stiff in the East. The Celtics had to get as far away from home as possible for an entire week to find out who they are. But road trips, as fine as they turn out to be, do have their limits. \u201cWe need to get out of here,\u201d Irving said. \u201cWe\u2019ve been here long enough. No disrespect to any California native, but we need to get out.\u201d * * * Shaun Powell has covered the NBA for more than 25 years. Bird erupted for a career-best 60 points in a 126-115 win against the Hawks . He was was unstoppable for most of the game to finish 22-for-36 from the floor. The Warriors, who entered as 17-point favorites and led by 16 in the first quarter, made their inevitable run. "},{"id":35072,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/about-last-night-3-9-19","date":"2019-03-10","title":"About Last Night: Giannis, Lopez lead Bucks to 50th victory","content":"Another game, another impressive performance for Giannis Antetokounmpo. The Kia MVP candidate had 26 points, 13 rebounds and six assists, and the Bucks beat the Hornets 131-114 for their NBA-best 50th victory. Charlotte led by 14 in the second quarter before Antetokounmpo came alive. Then Brook Lopez took over in the third quarter, scoring 18 of his 25 points as the Bucks seized control of the game. Lopez attacked smaller defenders at the rim every chance he got, getting in close for layups and dunks. He also went 10-of-10 from the stripe. \u201cHe\u2019s a very gifted, talented, unique player,\u201d coach Mike Budenholzer said of Lopez, who has taken two-thirds of his shots this season from beyond the arc. It\u2019s just the second 50-win season in the past 30 years for Milwaukee (50-16), which went 52-30 during the 2000-01 season and reached the Eastern Conference finals. The last time the Bucks were the first team to reach 50 wins (1973-74), they reached the NBA Finals. With LeBron James on a minutes restriction and other starters unavailable due to injuries, the latest chapter of the NBA\u2019s most storied rivalry was less than competitive. After Sunday\u2019s trip to Dallas, the Rockets play four straight home games, highlighted by a visit from the Warriors on Wednesday night."},{"id":35100,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/preemptive-extensions-free-agency","date":"2019-03-09","title":"Teams look for balance between rewarding stars early, playing free agency game","content":" A bunch of Milwaukee Bucks fans, and more than a few of the team\u2019s players, breathed a sigh of relief last week when news broke that point guard Eric Bledsoe had agreed to a four-year, $70 million contract extension. By locking up Bledsoe early, Milwaukee kept the muscled, two-way point guard off the free agent market this summer. The Bucks still have multiple players from their core nearing the end of their current deals \u2014 Khris Middleton, Brook Lopez, Malcolm Brogdon, Nikola Mirotic \u2014 so nailing down at least one of the key puzzle pieces around MVP candidate Giannis Antetokounmpo eases at least a little uncertainty for what comes after this happy 2018-19 season and postseason. They also lessened the downside by reportedly guaranteeing only $3.9 million of the $19 million due Bledsoe in the last year of the deal. Then again, the Bucks bid against no one in signing Bledsoe now rather than later. They committed to a guard who will be 32 in the final season of the extension, a guy who has helped his teams to just one playoff series in the past five seasons. In setting their own market for Bledsoe four months early, Milwaukee essentially traded one risk \u2014 losing the player without compensation in July \u2014 for another. Which seems to be happening a lot around the NBA these days. Maybe it\u2019s Cleveland and forward Kevin Love jumping at a four-year, $120 million extension last summer, a deal that won\u2019t start until next fall for a player who has missed 53 games this season and an average of more than 25 per season since he joined the Cavaliers in 2014. Or maybe it\u2019s Minnesota doing the same thing with Andrew Wiggins, rushing to give the never-All-Star a five-year , $146 million extension in October 2017.  It\u2019s bad enough that Love\u2019s $24 million salary, even if he\u2019s healthy for the remainder of the Cavs\u2019 schedule, annualizes out to $66 million for the games he\u2019ll actually play. No surprise that a 30-year-old veteran in his 11th season, with his history of injuries and ailments, would jump at the chance to guarantee early what might be his final (at least final big) NBA payday. Love at least is a five-time All-Star and affable personality who, at his best, is worthy of planting a flag with for a franchise in serious transition after LeBron James left last summer. It\u2019s worse that Wiggins\u2019 massive deal, for a player whose intensity and desire to fully tap his many skills has been suspect for years, looked like a mistake well before it kicked in for this season. If ever a player seemed to need the urgency of an open market \u2014 as a restricted free agent, still in Minnesota\u2019s control had he even reached that status last summer \u2014 it\u2019s Wiggins. Unless you think it\u2019s a good idea to purchase luxury cars with missing motors. Now consider Washington\u2019s John Wall, whose ruptured Achilles tendon likely will keep him out until deep into next season. That, finally, will be Year 1 of the four-year, $170 million max extension the Wizards signed with him back in July 2017. They assumed 100 percent of the risk, giving Wall a player option for the final 2022-23 season. Granted, he has been their best player for most of his NBA tenure, that essential piece in their, um, early eliminations and playoff misses. Now there\u2019s genuine concern that the quickness that literally has separated him from most of his peers might be gone to injury and surgery. What isn\u2019t gone is the contract obligation Washington faces. Sometimes, admittedly, these pre-emptive strikes work out nicely. The four-year, $100 million (that is, less than maximum) package the Bucks gave Antetokounmpo is a relative steal \u2014 and sets a bar for what other Milwaukee players, such as Middleton, might think about making, in terms of drawing a bigger salary than the team\u2019s essential talent. Blake Griffin\u2019s five-year, $173 million deal signed with the Clippers as a free agent in 2017 became Detroit\u2019s burden entirely when they traded for the All-Star forward. Fortunately for the Pistons, Griffin\u2019s game has grown in the interim, making the dollar amounts ($39 million in 2021-22 when he hits age 33) appear less onerous. Jumping the gun \u2014 out of a fear of losing players outright \u2014 is one thing. Getting out over one\u2019s skis to pre-empt restrictive free agency is an even shakier strategy. Portland did it with players, stuck because their departures wouldn\u2019t have opened up enough cap space to compete in the market otherwise. But who looks smarter now: Chicago for being \u201cpassive\u201d and matching the four-year, $78 million offer sheet Zach LaVine signed with Sacramento or Minnesota owner Glen Taylor for looking deep into Wiggins\u2019 eyes (that was a prerequisite for the Wolves boss) before handing over all that dough for still untapped potential? These tactics have been further complicated by the recent trend of some NBA stars \u2014 cornerstone players to whom franchises either had or would have committed their futures \u2014 to fuss and buck with time left on the fat contracts they receive.  Notice the escalation from Paul George serving notice in Indiana that he planned to leave to Kyrie Irving wanting a trade away from Cleveland, followed by Jimmy Butler undermining the Timberwolves at the start of this season to leverage his trade to Philadelphia, finally to Anthony Davis demanding out of New Orleans as the February trade deadline neared \u2014 with 1.5 seasons left on his Pelicans deal. The NBA long has been a supporter of players\u2019 movement when they reach free agency and have the same freedom to choose whatever employer, colleagues, geography or professional ambitions suit them. But while upholding their end of a bargain into which they willingly entered? Nope. It\u2019s an unforeseen consequence of the owners, in the most recent collective-bargaining talks, preferring shorter contracts as a way to protect against under performance and debilitating injuries (think Chicago\u2019s Derrick Rose). Now, with four-year contracts turned into two-, it\u2019s something that goes bump in the night to keep commissioner Adam Silver tossing and turning. No one questions the business strategy when it works; so far, Russell Westbrook\u2019s five-year, $205 million extension, signed in 2017, appears to have been a solid move. It essentially kept both him and George in Oklahoma City. But there are matching examples, a miscalculation for every success, throughout the league. And in between the two rules of free agency \u2014 \u201cnever let a star leave for nothing in return\u201d and \u201cnever bid against yourself\u201d \u2014 there is this enduring one: You\u2019re damned if you do and damned if you don\u2019t. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. Ingram has missed the Lakers\u2019 past two games after he reported shoulder pain following a morning shootaround last Monday. Lakers coach Luke Walton said earlier this week that Ingram had pain whenever he attempted to lift his arm. A deep venous thrombosis is a condition caused by a blood clot, frequently accompanied by swelling and pain in a limb. The condition usually occurs in legs, but can occur in the upper body. Blood clots have affected several prominent athletes including Chris Bosh and Serena Williams in recent years. Bosh eventually retired due to blood clotting issues, but many other athletes have continued to play. Ingram is averaging a career-best 18.3 points and 5.1 rebounds per game along with 3.0 assists. Their hair is pulled tight, their uniforms are buttoned down. And the 100-yard stares they\u2019ve developed serve them well when some critic or comedian in the stands gets a little too boisterous. As first-year NBA referees, they\u2019re all about the job. As first-year female NBA refs, they know they\u2019d better be. But then a coach calls a timeout, everybody exhales and the reality of where they are and what they\u2019re doing hits both of them. If not for the whistles clenched between their teeth, you might even see them smile. \u201cI don\u2019t find it intimidating at all,\u201d Sago, 29, said recently about the big stage on which she\u2019s working this season, after climbing the referees\u2019 ladder from high school to college to G League games. \u201cI love it. It really gets me going. It starts with the starting lineups, they turn the lights off and get the flames going. And during the game, when the home team goes on a run, everybody\u2019s on their feet. And I\u2019m like, \u2018This is awesome.\u2019  \u201cI\u2019m a big banners girl. During a timeout, I love looking at all the banners, all the history. You let it all soak in. I love the arenas, the fans. It fuels me. Bring it on. I want more.\u201d Said Moyer-Gleich, whose career timeline has paralleled Sago\u2019s: \u201cThese four months have been incredible. So much more special than I ever thought it would be. Growing up playing basketball, I\u2019d been a gym rat, all day, every day for most of my 31 years. But walking into those NBA arenas and putting on my uniform and standing on the court when fans are piling in \u2026 even 25 games in, it makes my heart flutter so much. How blessed am I that this is my job?\u201d Both got the call in November from Michelle Johnson, senior vice president and head of referee operations. Along with Mousa Dagher , Matt Myers and Phenizee Ransom , Sago and Moyer-Gleich were among the five G League referees promoted to full-time NBA status. Brandon Adair already had been added, giving the league six first-year refs for 2018-19. So far, the \u201crookies\u201d have done about as well as expected, an encouraging sign and a demonstration that the NBA\u2019s budding minor league can be a reliable proving ground not just for players and coaches but for game officials as well.  \u201cOur coaching feedback has been positive,\u201d said Monty McCutchen, VP and head of referee development and training. \u201cYour first year in the NBA, regardless of your gender, it can be a difficult league to gain acceptance in. We understand and take sort of a long view toward the growth of our officials. What our organizations and coaches and players want is accuracy and will and strength and courage. As we are finding out in many facets of life, those characteristics of life are not gender-specific.  \u201cWe recognized the need for the pipeline to grow in this way. Natalie and Ashley have shown the proper skill set, along with the intestinal fortitude, to take this next step. I\u2019m hopeful that in a very few years it won\u2019t seem out of place, it won\u2019t seem special.\u201d McCutchen wants people to know that \u2014 as \u201cprogressive\u201d as the NBA\u2019s reputation is, and as willing to lead the way among major sports leagues in trends and social issues \u2014 Sago and Moyer-Gleich got this opportunity on merit. They are the fourth and fifth female referees in NBA history, following in the footsteps of Dee Kantner (1997-2002), Violet Palmer (1997-2016) and still-active Lauren Holtkamp , 38. \u201cAshley and Natalie both earned their spots because they were among the best available for the spots that were open,\u201d McCutchen said. \u201cThey happened to be women. \u201cWe can\u2019t sit here and talk about a program of integrity, and then turn around and not honor what it would mean to hire the best officials. I don\u2019t want to be a part of that.\u201d With Holtkamp still rehabbing after suffering a knee injury last spring, Sago and Moyer-Gleich are conspicuous on NBA courts for obvious reasons. But their experiences, like their reception by an overwhelming majority of players and coaches, have been almost by-the-book what newbie refs get regardless of gender. This is, after all, 2019, not 1999 or 1979 or 1959. Neither spoke of any resistance related to being female. Quite the opposite in some cases, in fact.  \u201cIt\u2019s really neat when some of the players come up during warmups, shake your hand and say, \u2018Welcome to the league,\u2019 \u201d Sago said. \u201cThat means a lot. It\u2019s a stepping stone to have that player-referee communication. Just to get that started, talking and communicating with players, makes me comfortable that, \u2018Hey, I can probably talk to this guy throughout the game.\u2019 That\u2019s really nice. \u201cThe coaches have welcomed me. Those might be more heated interactions throughout my career and I\u2019ve expected that and been ready for that. But so far everything\u2019s been good. Lots of learning and growing moments, which is the beauty of this job.\u201d  Both Sage and Moyer-Gleich advanced last season to the G League\u2019s \u201cenhanced\u201d program for referees. That guarantees them work in at least 50 games, carrying a monthly stipend that serves as \u201csalary\u201d compared to the per-game compensation they get piecing together schedules of G League and NCAA games. They both still work a few games each month in the minor league, serving as crew chiefs there and mentoring even less experienced officials who are vying for similar careers. As of late February, each had worked about 20 NBA games this season and seven or eight in the G League. Sago, from Farmington, Mo., got her start as an amateur athlete whose father was refereed high school basketball. He would take her along to his games, and her window into the NBA began as someone who watched the refs as much as most fans focus on the players. She has pinch-me moments now feeling like a peer to veteran officials such as Mike (Duke) Callahan or Ken Mauer whom she used to notice on TV. Moyer-Gleich, from Harrisburg, Pa., led her Millersville University team to the Division II \u201cSweet 16\u201d in 2010 before working five years of high school games in Pennsylvania. She was an adjunct professor at Messiah College teaching a sports-wellness course when talent scouts for the NBA\u2019s officiating program noticed and sought her out.  That was her door into the career she cherishes now, one supported and easily understood by her husband Johnee Gleich, who referees men\u2019s NCAA games . As with Sago, the next bit of pushback will be her first. Whether withstanding an in-the-moment critique from San Antonio Spurs coach Gregg Popovich or telling Golden State Warriors forward Draymond Green exactly why she called what she called, Moyer-Gleich hasn\u2019t had to flinch. \u201cThe players have been fantastic. The coaches have been fantastic,\u201d she said. \u201cNo one has said anything derogatory in any way about being a female. I\u2019ve had several players come up to me saying, \u2018It\u2019s about time. I have daughters\u2026\u2019 It\u2019s a testament to our league and the players we have to be so professional, and accepting and open. \u201cI\u2019m sure at some point the honeymoon will be over. It will wear off when there are tough games and tough plays, but that\u2019s part of our jobs. But for the first four months, they have been amazing.\u201d Chicago Bulls coach Jim Boylen has had both women work his team\u2019s games. He\u2019s a coaching lifer who came across stylistically as part throwback, part dinosaur when he replaced Fred Hoiberg earlier this season. But not in this area. \u201cThey\u2019re both excellent young officials,\u201d Boylen said. \u201cGreat attitudes and work ethics and eye contact. And toughness. You can get on \u2018em and they take it.  \u201cI don\u2019t think gender plays a role at all. It\u2019s basketball, y\u2019know? I see an official out there and I respect that. I know that person has worked their ass off to get here, just as we all have.\u201d Boylen did admit to one gaffe involving the two newcomers. \u201cI\u2019ve called them the wrong name,\u201d he said, laughing. \u201cI called Ashley \u2018Natalie\u2019 and Natalie \u2018Ashley.\u2019\u201d That\u2019s OK, though. Sago said, for example, when asking a player to tuck in his jersey, she\u2019ll hear a reflexive \u201cYes sir\u201d occasionally. \u201cI\u2019m always around the guys. It doesn\u2019t hurt my feelings,\u201d she said. The logistics of accommodating female referees is old news for the NBA, with dressing rooms and privacy readily available in the wake of Kantner, Palmer and Holtkamp. It\u2019s a pretty enlightened group of colleagues, too, to hear the two tell it. \u201cWe\u2019re new hires,\u201d Moyer-Gleich said, \u201cand a lot of the guys probably feel a little protective over us, whether it\u2019s in a sister way or a daughter way. Guys who we didn\u2019t have a relationship with went out of their way at the start to text me after games saying \u2018Good job\u2019 with this or that. It makes you feel a sense of \u2018I belong here.\u2019 And that perception, if that\u2019s the aura you\u2019re giving off, the players and the coaches will buy into that.\u201d There are no special rules regarding physical skirmishes between players, either. \u201cReally, it comes down to safety,\u201d Moyer-Gleich said. \u201cI had a scrum in December with the Bulls and OKC. It\u2019s scary \u2014 these are big guys, so me trying to separate them is not going to do too much. But I also feel when players hear a female\u2019s voice, sometimes that calms them down a little bit.\u201d It\u2019s the same, too, when the heat is restricted to verbal exchanges. Sago considers herself a \u201cpeacemaker.\u201d So even if the goal is for men and women referees to be essentially interchangeable, she wonders if a little less testosterone in the moment can have some benefit. \u201cSome males, they go right back at [a player or coach]. They want to go head-to-head sometimes,\u201d she said. \u201cI feel like, being female, we can defuse things a little easier and quicker.\u201d Ultimately, the story of Sago\u2019s and Moyer-Gleich\u2019s first NBA seasons is little different from any of the other rookie refs. Same stripes, same whistles, same orientation process to the speed, skills, arenas, travel, personalities and oversight that\u2019s as rigorous as anything air-traffic controllers face. The proof mostly is in the reffing, where it should be. As for feeling like pioneers here in 2019? \u201cWe were so lucky to have Violet and Dee and Lauren pave the way,\u201d Moyer-Gleich said. \u201cFor this next generation where having female referees in men\u2019s games won\u2019t be a shock, they may consider us pioneers. I don\u2019t, [but] I definitely want to inspire the next generation of females and leave a legacy.\u201d * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. The views on this page do not necessarily reflect the views of the NBA, its clubs or Turner Broadcasting."},{"id":35170,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/kia-mvp-ladder-week-21-2018-19","date":"2019-03-08","title":"Kia MVP Ladder: Five future MVP types to keep an eye on ...","content":" The future. It\u2019s not something most in the NBA ponder during the intense heat of a late-season playoff push . Everything is compartmentalized. You\u2019re trying to win the next possession, the next quarter, the next game. Anything beyond what\u2019s right in front of you cannot claim your focus. The Draft lottery, the playoffs, awards season, the 2019 Draft , free agency \u2026 everything has its time and place on the NBA calendar. But what if we looked into the future, and not to just the summer of 2019, but three, four or five years from now? What does the landscape look like? Who takes over for the current generation of superstars? Are they even on our radar right now? I posed those questions to two NBA executives, one from each conference, two advance scouts and one college scout in search of a picture of what the future of the Kia Race to the MVP Ladder might look like five years from now. Surely, we\u2019ll have some familiar faces, current stars entering the primes of their careers who will no doubt remain relevant in the MVP conversation. Guys like Giannis Antentokounmpo, Joel Embiid, Karl-Anthony Towns, Anthony Davis and Ben Simmons \u2013 all leaders of the new school of superstars. But who are the budding superstars with the right combination of star power and status \u2013 i.e., guys on teams whose team success could accelerate things \u2013 and quickly rise the Kia MVP ranks? Here are the five players who garnered the most mention from our future MVP panel: Donovan Mitchell, Utah Jazz: An undersized but powerful dynamo, Mitchell\u2019s uniquely positioned to make the move into the MVP ranks in the future. That\u2019s based as much on his own individual brilliance and offensive skill as it is his headlining a team that could be a contender soon. \u201cHe\u2019s got a skill-set that will allow him to be a high-level dictator of outcomes for his team on a regular basis,\u201d said an Eastern Conference executive. \u201cGo back and look at the way he played against [Houston] in the playoffs last season. That\u2019s what MVP candidates in our league do when the lights are brightest. He went toe-to-toe with James [Harden] in that series as a rookie. The better shooter he becomes, the more difficult he\u2019ll be to stop. Because he\u2019s already a nightmare matchup because he plays so much bigger than his actual size. His competitive drive is off the charts and the framework they\u2019ve provided in Utah is rock-solid. He\u2019s a great fit there.\u201d Luka Doncic, Dallas Mavericks:  He knows a little something about this MVP business, having already earned some serious hardware before his breakout rookie season with the Mavericks. But how will he and Kristaps Porzingis fit in the coming years? And will he continue to thrive with another elite young talent requiring the ball to be effective? \u201cThe thing about his game that sets him apart is that he\u2019s got the skills to be just as good on or off the ball,\u201d a Western Conference advance scout said. \u201cIf he was just a scorer and had to survive on that alone, I don\u2019t know that he\u2019d be nearly as dangerous. He\u2019s going to be a triple-double threat with his size, playmaking ability and feel for the game. He came in with a polish that takes years for some guys to put on their game, if they ever do. But it all depends on the chemistry he and KP develop. If it works, he\u2019ll be a monster for years to come.\u201d Jayson Tatum, Boston Celtics:  The talent logjam in Boston could be alleviated this summer, via trades and\/or free agency, giving Tatum a clear path to assume the superstar role some think he\u2019s destined for with the Celtics. That is, of course, if he\u2019s not a part of the offseason player movement. \u201cI\u2019ve pounded the table about this for years, since I first scouted him at Duke. He has the most translatable offensive skill-set for the league of any prospect I\u2019ve scouted the past five years,\u201d said a college scout for an Eastern Conference team. \u201cYou either have the physical tools or you don\u2019t. The size, the basketball IQ and the understanding of how to attack his defender \u2026 he\u2019s got advanced level stuff in that regard. KD [Kevin Durant] was the same way at roughly this same stage of his career and drove himself to greatness with a work ethic that\u2019s second to none. If Tatum has that same kind of drive in him \u2014 and I don\u2019t know if he does, but I\u2019ve heard great things about the kid \u2014 he\u2019s your guy.\u201d De\u2019Aaron Fox, Sacramento Kings:  Fox is the pilot for a Sacramento team that could be ready for takeoff in the next season or two and the best two-way player on this list. He\u2019s also one of the fastest players in the league and an underrated athlete who has already shown signs of 20-10 potential. \u201cI love him. Absolutely love his game and how hard he goes,\u201d said a Western Conference executive. \u201cEnd to end, he\u2019s got the kind of acceleration that only a couple of guys in the league can match, like Russ [Westbrook] and John [Wall] showed early on. That\u2019s sort of speed, and the ability to control games with tempo, is a game changer when utilized properly. He\u2019s a much better shooter now than I thought possible after seeing him as a rookie. But he\u2019s worked at it. He\u2019s got so much room to grow, physically and just his game overall. And he\u2019s already a guy you have to account for when you prepare for the Kings. Like I said, I love the kid. He just plays with the right spirit.\u201d Trae Young, Atlanta Hawks:  Those lofty Stephen Curry comparisons that accompanied Young into the league seemed so far-fetched early on this season, when the rookie point guard struggled with his shot and his niche. His late-season surge of high-scoring, high-assist performances has added some credence to the idea that Young could be the leader of a Warriors-inspired revival in Atlanta. \u201cIt was easy to take advantage of him early on because he wasn\u2019t strong enough to do much of anything against guys at his position,\u201d an Eastern Conference advance scout said. \u201cTo his credit, he figured out how to create situations he could take advantage of to get it done. I still cringe at some of the shots he takes. But, and hear me out on this, his best skill isn\u2019t his shooting. The thing that will make him is his ability to drill you in the pick and roll with his passing. He masters that and his shooting, his ability to generate offense for himself and his team goes off the charts. I think he\u2019s a next-level passer more than anything. To me, that\u2019s what sets his game apart.\u201d * * * The top five this week in the 2018-19 Kia Race to the MVP Ladder: * * * Last week:  No. 2 Season stats:  27.1 points, 12.6 rebounds, 6.0 assists, 1.5 steals, 1.4 blocks In Thursday night\u2019s rout of the Indiana Pacers , Antetokounmpo reminded anyone that hasn\u2019t paid close attention just how difficult it will be to deal with his Bucks at home in the playoffs. His supporting cast is filled with shooters who possess the length and athleticism to accentuate all that he brings in coach Mike Budenholzer\u2019s attack. Any lingering doubts about the Bucks\u2019 validity as the league\u2019s best team this (regular) season should have been silenced weeks ago. Antetokounmpo\u2019s been good enough on his own to inspire Bucks fans to dream big in regards to what this season can bring. The in-season additions of both Nikola Mirotic and now Pau Gasol have fortified the roster with even more length, shooting and championship-level seasoning. \u201cThe Greek Freak,\u201d fellow All-Star Khris Middleton, Malcolm Brogdon and Eric Bledsoe are providing the frontline star power needed to power any team harboring championship ambitions. It\u2019s clear that the Bucks fancy themselves a championship-ready outfit, given their willingness to go all-in for 2018-19. Last week:  No. 1 Season stats:  36.7 points, 7.5 assists, 6.5 rebounds, 2.2 steals Harden shows no signs of letting up, which works for both his MVP candidacy as well as the Rockets\u2019 goal of climbing as high as they can in the West. Harden\u2019s special season continues with a Friday night visit from the Philadelphia 76ers ( 8 ET, ESPN ), yet another showcase opportunity for the reigning Kia MVP against an elite East opponent. Harden dials up some of his best for the biggest games. And the Rockets are measuring themselves against all of the other contenders down the stretch. That\u2019s why you should expect to see Curry and Co. at their best against a Nuggets team that presents a direct challenge to their hold on the No. 1 spot out West. Whatever issues the Warriors have now, there is plenty of time to fix them between now and the playoffs. Curry\u2019s own assessment \u2014 that the Warriors are \u201cstruggling\u201d only in the context of the extremely lofty expectations they have for themselves \u2014 makes perfect sense. Damian Lillard turned in an epic performance, finishing with 51 points, nine assists and five rebounds. The end of regulation was filled with drama. Jusuf Nurkic took an elbow to the face from George, but George was not called for a foul. George missed his layup attempt on the play, but with Nurkic on the floor, Terrance Ferguson grabbed the rebound and scored with 13.1 seconds left to put Oklahoma City up 113-111. Moments later, George and Nurkic jawed at each other and drew technical fouls with 4.4 seconds left. Because it was Nurkic\u2019s second technical, he was ejected. Thunder coach Billy Donovan then chose reserve Skal Labissiere to shoot the Blazers\u2019 two free throws in place of Nurkic. He missed both, but a loose-ball foul was called on the Thunder and Al-Farouq-Aminu made both shots to tie it at 113 with 2.9 seconds left. After a turnover by the Thunder, the game went to overtime, where Westbrook scored eight points, including the go-ahead 3-pointer. The victory gave the Thunder a four-game series sweep of the Blazers this season, and pulled them ahead of Portland and Houston, who entered the night in a three-way tie for No. 3 in the West. Instead, Myles Turner was credited with a block and the Pacers went the other way, with Bojan Bogdanovic drilling a 3-pointer. Three-point game. Antetokounmpo stayed under the basket in disbelief for a moment before getting up and running down an official to voice his displeasure, immediately getting assessed a technical. Milwaukee responded with a 13-0 run. Antetokounmpo started the spurt with a dunk and finished it with another. Bucks coach Mike Budenholzer said the entire team fed off the technical foul. \u201cIt felt like the whole arena, his teammates, I think everybody was probably somewhat frustrated, but I think that frustration got channeled into a great energy,\u201d Budenholzer said. The Bucks never looked back on their way to a 117-98 victory , halting their first losing streak of the season. Antetokounmpo finished with a team-high 29 points and 12 rebounds. The Los Angeles Lakers superstar scored his 32,293rd point on a driving layup in the second quarter against the Denver Nuggets, getting fouled in the act and hitting the ensuing free throw. This achievement was particularly special to James, who grew up in Ohio idolizing Jordan. James tweeted his excitement about the milestone shortly before the game: \u201cCan\u2019t even front. This is going to be UNREAL!! Wow man.\u201d James began the night needing 13 points to reach the mark. He now trails only Kareem Abdul-Jabbar (38,387 points), Karl Malone (36,928) and Kobe Bryant (33,643). Under direction from the NBA, the Lakers waited until the next timeout to honor James, who got a standing ovation from his new Los Angeles fans during a tribute video. James, still engrossed in the game, barely acknowledged the milestone after receiving hugs from several teammates. James finished with 31 points, seven rebounds and seven assists, but powerful Denver beat the Lakers 115-99 . >  Fellow players react to LeBron\u2019s milestone From the very first day James picked up a basketball, he wanted to be like Mike. James and his closest friends idolized Michael Jordan while they grew up together on Akron\u2019s competitive playgrounds and tough streets. As James grew into a tantalizing basketball prospect capable of jumping from high school to the NBA as the No. 1 draft pick, he studied, imitated and drew profound inspiration from Jordan\u2019s tongue-wagging dunks, that fadeaway jumper, his competitive fire \u2013 even the little details of the way Jordan wore his sneakers and shorts. James proudly put No. 23 on his back as soon as he could get it as a high school sophomore. He has never taken it off. \u201cHe was everything,\u201d James said. When James surpassed Jordan\u2019s career points total Wednesday night, he did it in a pair of Nikes with \u201cThank You M.J.\u201d written neatly on the side. That tribute doesn\u2019t begin to encompass what Jordan\u2019s radiance meant to a youngster who dreamed of finding a better life through basketball. \u201cM.J. was an inspiration,\u201d James said. \u201cM.J. was the lightning in a bottle for me, because I wanted to be like him.\u201d James has grown into a 34-year-old man with three championship rings and a redoubtable 16-year NBA career. Yet he is still in awe of Jordan, who has represented the apex of achievement for his entire life. He bettered Jordan\u2019s scoring mark with another variation of the brute physical brilliance that defines his playing style \u2013 a style that was influenced by Jordan\u2019s combination of grace and aggressiveness. His struggling Lakers rallied late on Wednesday, but dropped their fourth straight game in a season that appears to be lost. Every loss eats at James, yet he fidgeted in front of his locker afterward with the anxiousness of a rookie, still buzzing over the enormity of the moment in his life. >  More scoring milestones from LeBron\u2019s career \u201cWhen you\u2019re an inner-city kid from Akron, Ohio, like myself and my guys growing up, you look for anything that can inspire you,\u201d James said. \u201cYou\u2019re always just up against the numbers of failing. The percentages of guys like myself \u2013 single-parent household, only child, underprivileged \u2013 (the chance of) making it out is not high at all. M.J. had a lot to do with me making it out, along with my mother, along with the city itself, along with the Little League coaches I had. But Mike had no idea what he was doing for a kid that was growing up a 45-minute flight away from Chicago, where he was putting in that work.\u201d James acknowledged he missed most of the tribute video aired at Staples Center during the timeout after his milestone points. That\u2019s because he was crying into a towel on the Lakers\u2019 bench. James is among the last active players who were old enough to witness Jordan in his prime with the Chicago Bulls. Little LeBron would buy packs of basketball cards hoping for a Jordan, and he would study every aspect of Jordan\u2019s game, down to the way he wore his calf sleeve turned slightly inside-out so that the red lining showed. James couldn\u2019t afford Air Jordans, but he remembers walking through the mall and marveling at the pristine red-and-black shoes. They inspired him to dream of having his own shoes, a natural result of being the star he hoped to become. \u201cYou guys have no idea what M.J. did for me and my friends growing up,\u201d James said. \u201cJust in a sense of, some days where you just don\u2019t feel like you\u2019re going to make it to the next day where I grew up, because of everything that\u2019s going on. Like I wrote on my shoes today, I thank M.J. more than he would ever know. I\u2019ve got to carry it on to the next kid. Hopefully I can inspire the next kid.\u201d James is an inspiration to a generation now, many of whom have already reached the NBA. Several of the Nuggets spoke of idolizing James while they grew up, and his own Lakers locker room includes teammates playing alongside one of their boyhood heroes. \u201cI never saw Michael live,\u201d said Moritz Wagner, the Lakers\u2019 21-year-old German rookie center. \u201c(James) is the G.O.A.T. of our generation.\u201d James trails only Kareem Abdul-Jabbar (38,387 points), Karl Malone (36,928) and Kobe Bryant (33,643) on the scoring chart. He seems likely to become the top scorer in NBA history if major injuries don\u2019t cloud his next three seasons with the Lakers \u2013 but those moments just might not resonate as profoundly for James. \u201cTo see where he is now is remarkable,\u201d said Denver coach Michael Malone, an assistant coach with the Cavs from 2005-10. \u201cHe makes everybody around him better. \u2026 He made us look like really good coaches in Cleveland. I know that LeBron James is arguably one of the greatest ever to lace them up, and this is a great accomplishment in his career.\u201d Five of the top six scorers in NBA history played for the Lakers, who signed James as a free agent last summer. Although James has only played 47 games for Los Angeles, he has charged up the career scoring chart in that time: He passed Dirk Nowitzki for sixth place in October, and he passed Wilt Chamberlain for fifth in November. He would have hit this milestone earlier in the winter, but a midseason groin injury on Christmas sidelined him for 17 games over five weeks \u2014 the longest injury absence of his career. This achievement also might be the biggest highlight of a disappointing season with the Lakers (30-35), who have fallen to the fringe of playoff contention. The struggles bother James, who has played in the last eight NBA Finals and hasn\u2019t missed the postseason since 2005. But James still seems relatively content with his move to Hollywood, where he is closer to his burgeoning career as an entertainment mogul. He has spoken repeatedly of patience with the young Lakers while the club attempts to land a second superstar to turn James\u2019 next team into a title contender. Even if this season is a failure, James will never forget the night when he made a historic connection with his childhood hero. \u201cIt\u2019s crazy,\u201d James said. \u201cMy high school best friends, they text me and they can\u2019t believe it. I can\u2019t believe it either. "},{"id":35175,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/one-team-one-stat-blazers-guards-stronger-together","date":"2019-03-07","title":"One Team, One Stat: Blazers best with Lillard, McCollum together","content":" * Tonight on TNT:  Thunder vs. Blazers, 10:30 ET After getting swept as a No. 3 seed in the first round of the 2018 playoffs, the Portland Trail Blazers ran it back for another year behind a core of Damian Lillard, C.J. McCollum and Jusuf Nurkic. And once again, the Blazers are in the mix for a top-four seed in the Western Conference. After a 5-2 road trip coming out of the All-Star break, the Blazers are in a three-way tie with the Houston Rockets and Oklahoma City Thunder for third place in the West. The Blazers don\u2019t have the easiest remaining schedule of those three teams (Houston does), but they don\u2019t have the toughest either (Oklahoma City does by far). And they\u2019ll have their most important game left on their schedule in their building on Thursday, when they host the Thunder. Here\u2019s one number to know about the 2018-19 Blazers as they return home, play for third place in the West, and try to avoid a season sweep at the hands of the Thunder \u2026 The Blazers\u2019 starting lineup \u2014 Damian Lillard, C.J. McCollum, Maurice Harkless, Al-Farouq Aminu and Jusuf Nurkic \u2014 has played 668 minutes together. That\u2019s the most minutes for any five-man unit this season . Lineup continuity begins with health. Four of the Blazers\u2019 five starters \u2014 Lillard (1), McCollum (1), Aminu (0) and Nurkic (1) \u2014 have missed no more than one of the Blazers\u2019 64 games this season. Lillard, McCollum and Nurkic have all exhibited pretty remarkable durability in Portland. Harkless has missed 20 games, but the other aspect of lineup continuity is substitution patterns. And Blazers coach Terry Stotts has kept his starters on the floor together more than he had in previous seasons. The Blazers\u2019 starting lineup has played 17.6 minutes per game, the third-highest average among the 499 lineups that have played in at least 10 games together. In McCollum\u2019s fourth season as a starter, he and Lillard have already played 1,865 minutes together, second-most among league-wide pairings and almost as many as they\u2019d played together in any of the previous three seasons. Prior to this season, Stotts staggered Lillard and McCollum\u2019s minutes to ensure at least one of them was always on the floor. Typically, McCollum would sit the last few minutes of the first and third quarters, while Lillard did likewise the first few minutes of the second and fourth.  Last season, Lillard and McCollum shared the floor for an average of 7:50 in the first quarter. That was up from a little less than seven minutes over the previous two seasons. This season, Lillard and McCollum don\u2019t go to the bench at the same time, but McCollum stays on the floor longer in the first quarter and both guards are on the bench to start the second. On average, the two of have played 8:58 together in the first quarter and 9:18 in the third. The minutes with both Lillard and McCollum on the floor have never been better. The Blazers have outscored their opponents by 6.3 points per 100 possessions with both in the game, the pair\u2019s best mark (by a pretty wide margin) in their four seasons starting together. And since the Blazers have been getting more of those minutes, the improvement is felt even more. The issue is that the minutes with neither Lillard nor McCollum on the floor \u2014 minutes that *didn\u2019t really exist the previous three seasons \u2014 have not been good. Evan Turner has been the Blazers\u2019 second-unit playmaker for most of the season, and in 457 minutes with Turner on the floor and both Lillard and McCollum on the bench, the Blazers have been outscored by 7.8 points per 100 possessions . (* Not counting garbage time.)  The Blazers have been 14 points per 100 possessions better offensively with Lillard on the floor (scoring 114.8) than they\u2019ve been with him off the floor (100.8). With both Lillard and McCollum on the bench, the minutes spanning the first and second quarters have been rough. The Blazers have been outscored by 33 points in the last three minutes of the first quarter (when only six teams have been worse) and by 61 points in the first three minutes of the second (when only two teams have been worse). But that averages out to losing just 1.5 points per game in those six minutes. More often than not, the Blazers have been able to make up for it with those extended Lillard-McCollum minutes. As noted above, the guards average more minutes together in the third quarter (when the Blazers are a plus-7 for the season over the final three minutes) than they do in the first. Also, the neither-on-the-floor option isn\u2019t as bad when you look at last year\u2019s numbers. Last season, the Blazers outscored their opponents by 4.9 points per 100 possessions with Lillard and McCollum on the floor together and by 4.9 per 100 in 774 minutes with Lillard on the floor without McCollum. But they were outscored by 6.5 points per 100 possessions in 1,027 minutes with McCollum on the floor without Lillard. The month of December did test Stotts\u2019 resolve. The Blazers were outscored by 19.4 points per 100 possessions (and scored an anemic 94.7 per 100) in 116 minutes that month with Turner on the floor without either Lillard or McCollum. On Dec. 11 in Houston, the Blazers lost by seven in a game in which they were a plus-13 in more than 35 minutes with Lillard and McCollum on the floor together. (So they were outscored by 20 points in less than 13 minutes with both on the bench.)  The following night in Memphis , Stotts went back to staggering his starting guards\u2019 minutes so that one of them was on the floor for the entire game. The Blazers still lost, Stotts went back to using a full, five-man bench unit two nights later, and it rewarded him with quality minutes in a win over Toronto on Dec. 14 . There were more ugly bench nights after that, but Stotts has stuck with the original plan of sitting Lillard and McCollum together. The Blazers\u2019 most-used five-man bench unit \u2014 Turner, Seth Curry, Nik Stauskas, Zach Collins and Meyers Leonard \u2014 outscored its opponents by 1.5 points per 100 possession in its 196 minutes together. A month ago, the Blazers upgraded the Stauskas spot by trading for Rodney Hood . At the All-Star break, they also added Enes Kanter off the buyout market. Because Turner missed most of the Blazers\u2019 post-break trip, he\u2019s played in just two games with Hood and Kanter . But in 52 minutes so far with Hood on the court without Lillard or McCollum, the Blazers have outscored their opponents by 12.4 points per 100 possessions , with much better offensive numbers (115.2 scored per 100) than in those Turner minutes. We\u2019ll see if Stotts sits his two guards together in the playoffs. They\u2019ll each surely play additional minutes (Lillard has averaged 40.5 minutes in his 35 career playoff games ), and those can be used to either have one on the floor at all times or to increase the minutes that they\u2019re both on the floor together. There\u2019s certainly a case for the latter plan. But Zach LaVine did his best to grab the spotlight, scoring 39 points, capped by the game-winning layup with 1.6 seconds left, to lift the Chicago Bulls over the playoff-bound Philadephia 76ers 108-107 . Here is the winning drive and layup, which came from a Robin Lopez assist: LaVine\u2019s drive one-upped Jimmy Butler \u2014 for whom LaVine was traded back in 2015, and whose two free throws with 4.8 seconds remaining had given the 76ers a one-point lead in a hotly contested game that saw 20 ties and 19 lead changes. \u201cI saw the lane and I took it, \u201d said LaVine in his walk-off interview. He was fouled on the winning layup and purposely missed the free throw to consume time. On the 76ers\u2019 subsequent in-bounds play with only 0.5 seconds left, LaVine broke up the pass from Ben Simmons. But the game wasn\u2019t over. The clock had started early because Lopez appeared to tip the in-bounds pass, and the horn sounded before anyone caught the ball. But a review determined Lopez had not touched it and the final 0.5 seconds were replayed. The second inbounds pass wasn\u2019t handled by Butler, who finished with 22 points. Butler apparently wasn\u2019t too happy with his coach\u2019s final-second play calling. The young bulls are on an upswing since just before the All-Star break, having won six of their last 10. The Celtics are nowhere near the best team in the East, as they were cast in the offseason."},{"id":35221,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/michael-jordan-lebron-james-settling-goat-debate","date":"2019-03-06","title":"Jordan vs. LeBron, LeBron vs. Jordan ... either way, it's a taste test","content":"LOS ANGELES \u2014 Not since the biggest culinary quandary of our time and maybe all time \u2014 ketchup or mustard on a hot dog? \u2014 has a debate stirred emotions, drawn lines, fractured families and friends and caused even the sanest of souls to lose control: Who\u2019s the greatest player ever, Michael Jordan or LeBron James? It is never really settled. After making a fuss, the topic just sits there alone, like cold soup, ready to be reheated whenever people need a reason to get riled up. Everyone brings their own prejudices and preferences into the mix, trying to make a compelling case, frantically tossing in and out more numbers than a deadline-stressed accountant on April 14, pumping up their guy in order to put down the other guy . God created sports bars and water coolers and Skip Bayless for this very reason, to bring the mother of all basketball arguments to the forefront and mash it and mash it and mash it again into a paste without really coming up with a definitive conclusion. Each side digs in and no one is suddenly convinced and crosses over to the other side. Instead, MJ vs. LeBron seems certain to keep us divided as a nation. The tug-of-war rope is destined to gain tension once again, now that LeBron has passed Jordan for fourth on the NBA\u2019s all-time scoring list . Doing so took a bit of an ax to the Jordan throne, once believed to be untouchable and impenetrable. But fret no more. It is my job, as your humble referee, to introduce sanity and reason to explain, once and for all, the merits of this touchy G.O.A.T. debate and provide clarity and an answer that will, of course, satisfy no one. (Note: There\u2019s no reason to weigh the spin-off benefits of each player. Jordan boasts unprecedented clout as a product pitchman and LeBron has the reach via social justice and good deeds done through youth programs back home in Akron. None of that has a voice in pure basketball matters when it comes to the G.O.A.T. discussion, even though each player has made a mark beyond basketball.) Because the current climate demands it once again, we begin. As a society, we tend to be swayed by the moment, by the living and the breathing. Whatever is happening live and immediately leads us to dismiss anything or anyone that happened before ESPN. And among other things, this is why LeBron has momentum. He is right now. He is today. He is right in front of us. * Milestone baskets from LeBron\u2019s career Most of his career has taken place in the social media age, which makes the larger-than-life even larger than life and brings the power of persuasion to the masses worldwide. When LeBron sneezes, for example, laptop screens in China need to be wiped clean. But don\u2019t get it twisted; LeBron\u2019s obvious gifts and legacy are very real, no matter the medium. Let\u2019s list the honors, in order of weight: Twelve-time All-NBA first team, three Finals MVPs, three NBA titles (one in Cleveland, which should count double), four Kia MVPs, nine Finals trips in 15 seasons (including eight straight), 15 All-Star Games and five All-Defensive first team honors. He\u2019s the most unselfish scorer in league history, the only player to rank among the top 10 all-time in points and assists . Not since his rookie season has LeBron failed to lead his team in at least three major statistical categories. He has been, at times, the best point guard on the floor, the best shooting guard, the best power forward, the best small forward. His ability to lift mediocre teams consistently could be unmatched by anyone. The Cavs were poorly run through much of his time in Cleveland and that actually worked in his favor from an optics standpoint. His 2007 Finals team was arguably the weakest finalist ever, as the Cavs\u2019 Finals starters were a combination of Drew Gooden, Larry Hughes, Sasha Pavlovic, Daniel Gibson and Zydrunas Ilgauskas. He\u2019s had signature moments, such as his Game 5 outburst against the Detroit Pistons in the 2007 Eastern Conference finals, deflating the Boston Celtics with 45 points and 15 rebounds down 3-2 in the 2012 East finals. And the chase-down swat of Andre Iguodala in Game 7 of the 2016 Finals, among others. That\u2019s all good \u2026 but here are the nit-picks. LeBron has two stains on his legacy that can\u2019t be bleached: When he checked out against the Celtics in the 2010 East semifinals , and the 2011 NBA Finals when he never checked in against the Dallas Mavericks . Those are not up for debate. They are vitamins to the argument that LeBron wasn\u2019t wired like Jordan when it came to winning and producing in moments of truth. His Finals series record is 3-6, although to be fair, his teams weren\u2019t favored in four of those losses (in \u201907 vs. the Spurs and the last three Finals vs. the Warriors ). Also, in a far lesser vein, one of LeBron\u2019s three titles was secured when he missed a desperate 3-pointer in the clutch with his team facing elimination. The ball took a fortunate bounce off the rim and into the palms of Miami teammate Chris Bosh , who instinctively threw to Ray Allen and the rest is history . Another title was clinched when Kyrie Irving, not LeBron, dropped the trophy-winning shot in Game 7 of the 2015 Finals. Those are the details in the fine print that says LeBron\u2019s career is a fraction shy of perfection, and feel free to ignore that or concede as much. Which leads to this \u2026 The vast majority of sports fans born years too late to witness Babe Ruth were rewarded with Babe in baggy shorts from the mid-1980s to early 2000s. Jordan was the first NBA player to rise higher than the game , to tap into those parts of society that didn\u2019t follow sports or athletes, to become a cultural icon . Not to the extent of Muhammad Ali. But beyond anyone else of Jordan\u2019s generation, by far. He did so by winning championships and dropping memorable performances regardless of when and where he played. Folks in Sacramento, for example, were just as likely to see Jordan\u2019s brilliance in February as those in New York in May. Jordan didn\u2019t take nights off, didn\u2019t coast, didn\u2019t save himself. He got up for everyone. * Legends profile: Michael Jordan And Jordan spent 18 months of his prime \u2014 he was 30 at the time \u2014 dealing with a mid-life crisis that caused him to leave basketball for minor-league baseball . Hakeem Olajuwon and the Houston Rockets, who repeated as champs in Jordan\u2019s absence (\u201894 and \u201995), are forever grateful. Let\u2019s list the honors, in order of weight: Six NBA titles (while going six-for-six in The Finals), six Finals MVPs, five regular-season MVPs, 10 All-NBA first teams, 10 scoring titles, 14 All-Star Games, nine All-Defensive first team honors and one Defensive Player of the Year Award. In the post-Celtics dynasty era of the 1960s, he\u2019s the most efficient modern-day winner. He constantly raised his game to meet the stakes and never failed on the biggest of NBA stages. Jordan was the result of flair meeting talent and bloodthirsty spirit. The signature moments: His God-disguised-as-Michael-Jordan 63-point barrage on the Celtics in the first round of 1986. The \u201cShrug Game\u201d in the 1992 Finals. The \u201cFlu Game\u201d in the 1997 Finals. The legacy-calling, Bryon Russell-falling shot against Utah in The 1998 Finals (which was preceded by the most underrated play in championship history: stealing the ball off Karl Malone). Setting up Steve Kerr for the Finals clincher in 1997 . The reverse-flip against the Lakers in the 1991 Finals (Marv Albert voice: \u201c Oh, a spec-tac-ular move, by Michael Jordan!\u201d ). And, of course, the jumper over the fingertips of Craig Ehlo and the Cavs in 1989, among others. It\u2019s nearly impossible to chip away at Jordan\u2019s basketball imprint, especially once he got help on the Bulls and vanquished his boogeyman: the Isiah Thomas-led \u201cBad Boy\u201d Detroit Pistons . It\u2019s perhaps cruel to punish him for his two Washington Wizards seasons (2001-03), when Jordan was aging (and yet also an All-Star) and soon caved to nagging injuries caused by all those Bulls playoff runs. Still, Nick Anderson did pick him clean in a crucial playoff moment in 1995 when No. 45 made a hasty, midseason return to the NBA . Jordan \u2014 like other greats during his time \u2014 took advantage of an NBA watered down and weakened by the addition of expansion teams in the late \u201880s. And while on the subject of questionable competition, did he beat anyone special in the NBA Finals? The legacy of Jordan will be held to a higher elevation by the stubborn generation that saw him play, a generation that will not allow anything LeBron does (or anyone who comes after LeBron) to rob them of their moment. Likewise, if you ask a segment of the current generation about Jordan, some may ask innocently: \u201cYou mean the guy on the shoes?\u201d Which leads to \u2026 There are two: LeBron isn\u2019t done yet. And Jordan played in a different era. Shouldn\u2019t LeBron serve his entire career before anyone classifies him and what he\u2019s done in the NBA? Granted, he is on the back-nine and, at 34, struggling to put the Lakers in the playoffs. He also dealt with a semi-major injury for the first time ever, missing more than a month this season . But there\u2019s still another 2-3 years at a high level should he stay healthy, and if the Lakers add the right pieces around him this summer, maybe another Finals run, too. As for Jordan, keep in mind he played his career when the game was more physical and restrictive to offensive players. Defenders could hand-check Jordan without penalty. Had Jordan played in today\u2019s NBA with no hand-checking and freedom of movement, is it unrealistic to say he could\u2019ve averaged 40 points for a several-season stretch? In addition, the 3-pointer wasn\u2019t as widespread as now. Surely, Jordan \u2013 who averaged 1.7 3-pointers per game \u2014 would\u2019ve incorporated the shot more into his weaponry. Doesn\u2019t that make the LeBron-Jordan comparison more apples-to-oranges than you might think? Different players, different eras and a handful of conditional factors make this murkier than anyone wants to believe. Besides, the most accomplished crib-to-retirement basketball player was Kareem Abdul-Jabbar, who never got cut from his high school team (Jordan) or lost a state championship game (LeBron), won three NCAA titles in three years (freshmen were ineligible for the varsity, keeping him from going four-for-four), six NBA titles and sits atop the career scoring heap . So if we simply must have this debate, then upon further inspection, weighing all the important factors, judging the facts evenly, wiping away any biases and understanding the difficulty of it all, there can only be one answer, and it is \u2026 ketchup. * * * Shaun Powell has covered the NBA for more than 25 years.
